10.1016/j.jmp.2024.102872
Theories of assessment have different traditions in Psychometrics and Mathematical Psychology. Examples of the former are parametric, non-parametric, and multidimensional Item Response Theory (IRT, NIRT, and MIRT, see e.g.,  Van der Linden, 2016a, 2016b, 2018 ), and Cognitive Diagnostic Assessment 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         Although CDA also encompasses MIRT models, the acronym is preferred to Cognitive Diagnostic Models (CDMs) as it allows to properly spell out sentences like “KST, IRT, and CDA models”. 
                       (CDA, see e.g.,  Rupp et al., 2010; von Davier & Lee, 2019 ). An example of the latter is Knowledge Structure Theory (KST, see e.g.,  Doignon & Falmagne, 1999; Falmagne & Doignon, 2011 ). Scopes and aims of these frameworks are related yet different. IRT captures the probability of a correct response to an item through Item Response Functions (IRFs) based on real-valued latent traits. CDA and KST are instead mostly based on latent classes and classify individuals by means of the collections of problems that they are capable of mastering or the collections of attributes/skills that they possess.

In spite of the methodological differences, connections among these theories can be found in literature. A non-exhaustive review might include 
                         2 
                      
                      
                         2 
                         Models are directly addressed using their acronyms, a list of which is given in  Appendix B . 
                       the following works.  Schrepp (2005)  and  Ünlü (2006, 2011)  discussed the relation between KST and Latent Class Analysis (LCA);  Ünlü (2007)  suggested a generalization of Mokken’s scaling in NIRT based on KST;  Heller et al. (2015)  proved the equivalence of the DINO, DINA, and MS-DINA models in CDA with the CBLIM in KST;  Stefanutti (2006)  and  Noventa et al. (2019)  extended KST models to latent traits and showed their connections to IRT models (specifically, LKS and SLM in KST with respectively Divide-by-Total models and GRM/sequential models in IRT).  Noventa et al. (2019)  also showed that a combination of BLIM and SLM in KST captures and generalizes the assumption of Local Independence (LI) in IRT. Connections between CDA and IRT models abound in psychometric literature as the approaches are often mixed (see, e.g.,  DiBello et al., 2007; Hong et al., 2015; Junker & Sijtsma, 2001; von Davier, 2005; Wang & Chang, 2016 ). Many CDA models are instances of LCA, and are often seen as ‘located latent class’ versions of IRT models, a relationship evident in Structural LCA (see, e.g.,  Formann, 1982, 1985; Formann & Kohlmann, 1998, 2002 ). The same parametrization used in Formann’s Structural LCA was also applied by  Heinen (1993)  to discuss a direct link between restricted latent class models and discretized IRT models.

Although most models of these theories are Generalized Latent Variable Models (GLVMs, see, e.g.,  Bartholomew et al., 2011; Skrondal & Rabe-Hesketh, 2004 ), not all are members of exponential families. An example is provided by models involving guessing and ceiling parameters, also known as ‘left-side added models’ ( Thissen & Steinberg, 1986 ), like the 4-parameters model

Other examples of left-side added models in IRT are the 1PL-AG, Choppin’s extension to the 3PL, Samejima’s extension to the BNM, and Discrete Mixture models; in CDA, the Unified Model and the HO-PINC model; in KST, the LKS. These models require extensions of traditional GLVMs toward multiple class mixture models (see, e.g.,  Asparouhov & Muthén, 2020; Skrondal & Rabe-Hesketh, 2004 ), composite link functions ( Rabe-Hesketh & Skrondal, 2007 ), or multilevel mixture/latent class modeling ( Asparouhov & Muthén, 2008; Vermunt, 2008, 2011 ). The aim of the present manuscript is therefore the integration of GLVMs with a KST approach. Although IRT and CDA have greater popularity amongst psychometricians, KST is relevant within the Mathematical Psychology community. A unified framework allows to connect seemingly diverse notions and to identify a small set of primitives and operations from which models can be derived. In particular, we argue that KST, CDA, and IRT models have two primitives: the notion of ‘structure’, which captures the order relation between atomic elements of a given ‘sort’ (e.g., items or attributes), and the notion of ‘process’, which establishes a connection between structures. Specific models are derived by connecting processes and by setting assumptions on their conditional probabilities. Two operations on the latter are of particular relevance: ‘factorization’, which is associated to some independence assumption, and ‘reparametrization’, which is associated to the notions of kernel and link function. In the resulting taxonomy, models are instances of ‘blueprints’ identified by common assumptions that can be traced in literature and from which they can be derived via specific choices of structures, kernels, and link functions. The classification is unique up to reparametrization since suitable reparametrizations can render equivalent seemingly different models. Besides, common trends emerge: KST and early CDA approaches appear to be based on the same theory applied to elements of different sorts and with a focus on factorized conditional probabilities; modern CDA approaches appear to be discrete versions of IRT models, with the latter built on continuous structures, and with a focus on reparametrized conditional probabilities. Aside from specific models like those discussed in  Heller et al. (2015) , and theory-specific taxonomies for CDA (see, e.g.,  Rupp & Templin, 2008; Rupp et al., 2010; von Davier et al., 2008 ) or IRT (see, e.g.,  Sijtsma & Hemker, 2000; Thissen & Steinberg, 1986 ), there is up to our knowledge no comprehensive systematization of the models of these theories and of their equivalences.

The easiest example of similarity between conditional probabilities is given by conditional error parameters,  i.e. , the (constant) conditional probabilities of succeeding or failing by means of guesses and errors based on possessing or not knowledge and/or competence (conceived as mastered/learned notions or skills/attributes). Most frameworks follow the same diagram, illustrated on the left side of  Fig. 1 , which is a noisy channel in Information Theory ( Shannon, 1948 ), in which a signal is corrupted by noise (guesses and errors). By borrowing a terminology used by  Hutchinson (1991)  we call such a diagram a  process , and we argue that it is one of two primitives needed in a unified framework (the other one is the concept of  structure ). Specific models are derived by considering one or more processes combined together, and by putting constraints on their conditional probabilities.

The easiest constraint is that these conditional probabilities are held constant. This yields in IRT  guesses  and  errors  parameters

                          like in Eq.  (1)  and in the right diagram of  Fig. 1 . The IRF is, in its turn, the conditional probability of a second process that moves from a state of competence captured by the latent trait  
                            
                               θ

                          to a response

                         . In KST, the probability of guessing (or making a mistake) in solving an item is accounted for by the  lucky guess  ( careless error ) parameter that describes the conditional probability of (not) solving an item without (while) mastering it. Likewise, in CDA the  slipping  ( guessing ) parameter is the conditional probability of failing (solving) an item given that the required skills/attributes are (not) available.  Heller et al. (2015)  have shown the equivalence of these parameters in some CDA and KST models.  Noventa et al. (2019)  have shown the equivalence of KST and IRT conditional error parameters under the assumption of LI.

A fundamental distinction separates  blind/random  guessing from  informed/educated  guessing. A typical example for the latter is ruling out distractors in a multiple choice item. Informed guesses can occur if individuals manage to solve items without mastering all the required notions or possessing all the required skills, yet without resorting to pure chance. Similarly, errors due to confusion or misinformation do happen. Such a distinction is relevant to IRT models, in which concern has been raised for guessing parameters that differ from random guessing, particularly for low ability individuals (see, e.g.,  Chius & Camilli, 2013; De Ayala, 2008 ).  Hutchinson (1991)  distinguished between a  
                            p 
                         -process, in which ability-based solutions are searched, and a  
                            g 
                         -process, in which solutions are randomly guessed. These processes might be combined either sequentially or in parallel (see,  Hutchinson, 1991; Martin et al., 2006  for an overview of the topic). Such an approach has recently renewed interest in both the modeling of ability-based IRT guessing parameters and in the debate on the differences between single and multi-processes approaches to model psychological and cognitive behavior (see, e.g.,  Guo et al., 2021; Jiang et al., 2022; Martin et al., 2006; Park et al., 2015; van der Maas et al., 2011; Zhu et al., 2019 ). It is beyond the scope of this work to discuss the psychological relevance of a two-processes model interpretation of the response process, but it is argued that a sequential combination of  
                            p 
                         -process and  
                            g 
                         -process, as in the diagram of  Fig. 2 , provides the means and the vocabulary to systematize many KST, IRT, and CDA models. As in  Fig. 2 , individuals’ skills, attributes, or abilities (first layer) underlie which items are mastered or not (second layer), but these latter might not coincide with the observed data (third layer). In order to make the terminology as consistent as possible with the one used in KST, we adopt the term  Competence  for the skills, attributes, or abilities 
                            3 
                         
                         
                            3 
                            Although the terms ‘skill’, ‘attribute’, and ‘ability’ are respectively common in KST, CDA, and IRT contexts, they are often used interchangeably. We use skills and attributes to refer to dichotomous/polytomous skills and attributes, while we favor the term ‘trait’ over ability when referring to continuous latent variables. We also prefer the term ‘polytomous’ to characterize attributes with more than two categories as ‘categorical’ might mean both dichotomous or polytomous. Finally, we do not delve into the distinction between ‘mastering’ or ‘possessing’ a skill/attribute and the two terms will be here used interchangeably. 
                          possessed by individuals,  Knowledge  for the items that they are able to master, and  Response  for the actual data. The knowledge layer is called  ideal response pattern  in CDA and is interpreted as the response layer in IRT when left-side added parameters are not considered. The terms ‘competence’ and ‘knowledge’ are not meant to evoke any distinction between these two general notions, but just to apply the KST terminology. A distinction between  mastering  and  solving  is introduced in the same spirit as in KST, to distinguish between the latent knowledge and the observable responses.

The CDA-oriented nomenclature of informed guess and slip is used for the  
                            p 
                         -process, while the KST-oriented nomenclature blind guess and error is used for the  
                            g 
                         -process. The  
                            p 
                         -process connects the competence and knowledge layers establishing the relation between skills/attributes and items. An informed guess occurs if items are mastered in absence or partial presence 
                            4 
                         
                         
                            4 
                            ‘Partial presence’ is not meant in the sense of fuzzy membership, but it is modeled by allowing skills/attributes to have increasing degrees of mastery ( i.e. , categories) and by allowing, within the  
                                  p 
                               -process, for probabilistic transitions between different states of competence (see Section  4.2 ). 
                          of the adequate skills/attributes; similarly, slipping due to misinformation or confusion in presence of adequate skills can occur. The  
                            g 
                         -process connects the knowledge and response layers and, if the processes are independent, it is based on pure chance, like blind guessing, carelessness, slipping by chance, or making clerical mistakes. It should be stressed that the sequential approach of  Fig. 2 , with two independent processes, should not be understood as the ‘one and only’ from which all models can be derived. One can always opt for a different order of the processes (e.g., in parallel) and/or for different compositions of the layers (e.g., two competence layers). However, the combination of two processes as in  Fig. 2  appears to best systematize (see later  Table 4 ) most of the models since, even when the processes are parallel, one can distinguish a  
                            p 
                         -process and a competence-based  
                            g 
                         -process.

This first article provides both the foundations of a formal framework and a general overview of the taxonomy of models. Parts II and III focus instead on the derivation of the individual theories. As a general organizer, the following sections are meant to elaborate on the following five questions that can also be used by practitioners as a guideline to identify any model within these theories.

Q1 
                               Which atomic elements and structures are involved? 
                            
                            
                               Items, attributes, responses, and thresholds are  atomic elements  of different  sorts . Any dependence between the atomic elements of a given sort is captured by the primitive  structure  (see Section  2.1 ). The notational conventions used to merge the set-theoretic notation of KST and the vector-based notations of IRT and CDA are given in Section  2.2 . Competence, knowledge, and response structures are discussed in Section  3 . 
                            
                            
                               Q2 
                               How many processes are involved? 
                            
                            
                               Relations between structures are modeled by the primitive  process  (see Section  2.3 ). Processes can be combined in different ways. For instance, the model depicted in  Fig. 2  is formalized in Section  2.4  as a sequential combination of two processes. 
                            
                            
                               Q3 
                               Which assumptions are imposed on the processes? 
                            
                            
                               Different assumptions can be imposed on an arbitrary process. A fundamental one is whether the process is probabilistic or deterministic. While a probabilistic process provides a generalization of traditional latent class and latent trait models, a deterministic process is modeled by means of the KST concept of a  problem function , which generalizes the CDA concept of a  
                                     Q 
                                  - matrix  (see Section  4 ). Several common assumptions that can be traced in the literature are discussed in Sections  4 and 6 . 
                            
                            
                               Q4 
                               Is there some independence assumption on the process? 
                            
                            
                               Although Q4 is a specific case of Q3, it deserves to be investigated separately. If some form of independence is assumed over the atomic elements, the conditional probabilities of the processes are decomposed into a product over the probabilities of the single atomic elements. This operation, called  factorization  and discussed in Section  5.1 , is associated to the derivation of KST and early CDA models. 
                            
                            
                               Q5 
                               Which link function and kernel are chosen? 
                            
                            
                               Although Q5 is also a specific case of Q3, it deserves to be investigated separately too. A second operation,  reparametrization , alters the functional shape of the conditional probabilities via link functions and kernels as in GLVMs. This operation is discussed in Section  5.2  and is the one associated to the derivation of modern CDA models and general frameworks, as well as IRT models.

Which atomic elements and structures are involved?

Items, attributes, responses, and thresholds are  atomic elements  of different  sorts . Any dependence between the atomic elements of a given sort is captured by the primitive  structure  (see Section  2.1 ). The notational conventions used to merge the set-theoretic notation of KST and the vector-based notations of IRT and CDA are given in Section  2.2 . Competence, knowledge, and response structures are discussed in Section  3 .

How many processes are involved?

Relations between structures are modeled by the primitive  process  (see Section  2.3 ). Processes can be combined in different ways. For instance, the model depicted in  Fig. 2  is formalized in Section  2.4  as a sequential combination of two processes.

Which assumptions are imposed on the processes?

Different assumptions can be imposed on an arbitrary process. A fundamental one is whether the process is probabilistic or deterministic. While a probabilistic process provides a generalization of traditional latent class and latent trait models, a deterministic process is modeled by means of the KST concept of a  problem function , which generalizes the CDA concept of a  
                                     Q 
                                  - matrix  (see Section  4 ). Several common assumptions that can be traced in the literature are discussed in Sections  4 and 6 .

Is there some independence assumption on the process?

Although Q4 is a specific case of Q3, it deserves to be investigated separately. If some form of independence is assumed over the atomic elements, the conditional probabilities of the processes are decomposed into a product over the probabilities of the single atomic elements. This operation, called  factorization  and discussed in Section  5.1 , is associated to the derivation of KST and early CDA models.

Which link function and kernel are chosen?

Although Q5 is also a specific case of Q3, it deserves to be investigated separately too. A second operation,  reparametrization , alters the functional shape of the conditional probabilities via link functions and kernels as in GLVMs. This operation is discussed in Section  5.2  and is the one associated to the derivation of modern CDA models and general frameworks, as well as IRT models.

Given then two primitives (structures and processes) and two operations (factorization and reparametrization), a taxonomy encompassing KST, CDA, and IRT models is obtained and provided in  Table 4  in Section  6 , contingent on the assumptions made on the conditional probabilities in  Fig. 2 . The  
                            p 
                         -process is the blueprint for traditional GLVMs, which further differ w.r.t. choices of structure, kernel, and link function. Addition of a  
                            g 
                         -process yields models with left-side added parameters like Eq.  (1) . Competence-based left-side added parameters are obtained when the  
                            p 
                         - and  
                            g 
                         -processes are parallel, thus merging into a single process (based on a ‘multidimensional’ perspective on structures discussed in  Appendix A ). However, distinguishing between a  
                            p 
                         - and a  
                            g 
                         -process is still convenient for classification purposes. Due to the number of models encompassed, the derivation of specific KST, CDA, and IRT models is postponed to Parts II and III and only general guidelines are here provided. Part II focuses on dichotomous latent variable models in KST and CDA, while Part III focuses on polytomous and continuous latent variable models in IRT, KST, and CDA. Results and implications for the individual theories in light of the general frameworks are there given in more detail. A full introduction to the individual theories and models is not provided as the scope of the manuscript is to derive the very same models.

In order to formalize the diagram in  Fig. 2 , the present section introduces the primitives of  structure  and  process , establishes notational conventions to merge set-theoretical and vector-based notations, and finally introduces a sequential connection of two processes.

The term ‘memoryless’ has been borrowed to highlight that Eq.  (6)  is akin to the Markov Property: the output of the second process is not affected by the input of the first process ( i.e. ,  
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  X 
                                  | 
                                  C 
                                  , 
                                  K

                                  K 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         ). Eq.  (6)  is obtained by combining Eqs.  (4) and (5)  in such a way that it does not require a probability space encompassing all three structures, but only of the spaces underlying the processes  
                            
                               ( 
                               C 
                               , 
                               K 
                               ) 
                            
                          and  
                            
                               ( 
                               K 
                               , 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         . This highlights that the conditional independence of  
                            X 
                          and  
                            C 
                         , given  
                            K 
                         , is fundamental to have a sequential two-processes model. If dropped, a generalization to a space encompassing all three structures is obtained (see  Appendix A ) and the resulting model, which is given by Eq.  (A.1) , is actually a single process model

                            C

                            K

                               K

                                  |

                                  K 
                                  )

                               P

                                  X 
                                  | 
                                  K 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          corresponds to the assumption that the conditional probabilities do not vary across group-level classes, but with the difference that LI of  
                            
                               π 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  K 
                                  | 
                                  C 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          might not be assumed. In this sense, the M2PM is more general than the multilevel latent class model. Whether the latent classes  
                            K 
                          and  
                            C 
                          are actually hierarchical or cross-classified depends on the relation established between the different layers (see also problem function in Section  4 ).

A final remark: As already stressed in the introduction, the M2PM  (6)  should not be understood as the one and only model from which all other models can be derived, but as a combination of primitives that appears to best systematize most of KST, CDA, and IRT models and to provide the language to classify them. Eq.  (6)  acts like a blueprint for several families of models that can be obtained depending on (a) which structures are chosen for the different layers, and (b) which assumptions on the conditional probabilities are set.

Structures of different sorts can indeed be considered under the labels of Competence and Knowledge structures as long as their atomic elements are related to attributes or items in a broad sense. For instance, a domain of items is a straightforward interpretation, but one might use a chain like

                         0̸ 
                       ( 
                         D 
                      ) represents category zero (three). As the IRT intuition is that one needs to exceed a ‘threshold’ in order to change category, we call  item thresholds  ( attribute thresholds ) the atomic elements used to model a polytomous item (attribute). In general, structures based on domains of items, sub-items, or item thresholds are considered knowledge structures, while competence structures capture the relation between different attributes, sub-skills of a general attribute, or attribute thresholds. In order to distinguish domains and structures of items or attributes from those of the associated thresholds, we underline the latter.                             ) 
                         
                      .  Table 2  summarizes the notation used in the manuscript for the atomic elements, domain, and structures of different sorts.

Notice that, since the concept of structure allows for modeling relations between any sort of dichotomous atomic elements, results established for a collection of dichotomous items or attributes, also hold for a polytomous item or attribute in which the atomic elements are interpreted as thresholds ( Noventa et al., 2019 ). More in general, all results obtained for a particular sort of atomic elements can be transferred to other sorts, which might lead to different approaches to, e.g., groups of items like in testlets, item bundles, and parcels.

A final remark: Chains are necessary and sufficient for deriving the models listed in  Table 4  since a latent trait will be defined as the realizations of a random variable over a continuous chain (see Part III), but a structural approach can be used to generalize polytomous items and attributes to situations in which there are incomparable categories. While the present manuscript follows the IRT-inspired approach to polytomous items introduced in  Noventa et al. (2019) , more general extensions of the KST formalism to polytomous knowledge structures have been recently provided (see, e.g.,  Heller, 2021; Stefanutti et al., 2020, 2023; Wang et al., 2022 ) extending the seminal work by  Schrepp (1997) . In these works, the notion of item threshold is conceptualized as a ‘virtual item’.

As a final note, the M2PM of Eq.  (6)  provides a generic probabilistic relation between the competence and knowledge structures. One can elaborate such a model in three directions explored in the next sections: (a) one can embed the concepts of  
                            Q 
                         -matrix/problem function to provide a deterministic relation between attributes and items (which is a fundamental component of both KST and CDA); (b) one can allow individuals to slip and guess on attributes (which is a fundamental component of CDA); (c) one can further transform the conditional probabilities  
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  X 
                                  | 
                                  K 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  π 
                               
                               
                                  K 
                                  | 
                                  C 
                               
                            
                          via some additional operations.

                         Q

                         p 
                      -process, and we introduce the notion of  effective competence state , which captures the idea that the actual competence state of individuals might not correspond to the one associated to their observed responses.

                         . A fundamental concept in KST, CDA, and IRT, is LI. However, several definitions of LI have been given in literature and there is sometimes confusion between the different notions of ‘conditional independence’, ‘uni-dimensionality’, and ‘non-invasiveness’ of the item relations (see, e.g.,  Henning, 1989  for a discussion on the differences between these notions.). Besides, in addition to traditional forms of LI like conditional probabilistic independence (strong form) or conditional covariance (weak form), generalizations like essential independence ( Stout, 1990 ) or local non-negative dependence ( Holland, 1981 ), among others, are available. For a discussion of LI in the context of KST and IRT models, the reader is referred to  Noventa et al. (2019) . For a discussion on the inadequacy of LI as a definition of latent variables, the reader is referred to  Bollen (2002) . In what follows, we refer to the strong notion of LI as an exemplification of conditional independence. As shown in Section  6 , LI is the condition underlying most CDA and IRT models, while GLI is a condition that allows (a) to generalize classic CDA and IRT models to situations in which LI is violated, and (b) to treat models for polytomous items or polytomous attributes like models for dichotomous ones by using thresholds rather than items and attributes. As a final remark, the reader is referred to  Appendix A  for a discussion of an additional form of probabilistic independence that pertains to many-sorted structures and parallel multiple processes.

Link functions are an essential feature of Generalized Linear Models (GLMs) and GLVMs ( Bartholomew et al., 2011; Skrondal & Rabe-Hesketh, 2004 ) and their role as a means for reparametrization of the model probabilities, which allows to connect LCA and IRT models, was explored in Structural LCA (see, e.g.,  Formann, 1982, 1985 ).                             g 
                         -process is considered). Once applied to IRT, a choice of logit or probit link function yields traditional dichotomous IRT models, while a choice of log function yields Divide-by-Total polytomous models.

In the following, the results are briefly discussed, but the reader is referred to Part II for the derivation of KST and CDA models based on dichotomous latent variables, and to Part III for IRT, KST, and CDA models based on polytomous and continuous latent variables.

The families listed in  Table 5  are like ‘blueprints’ from which the models in  Table 4  are derived. Models within the same family are equivalent up to choices of specific sorts of structures, link functions, and kernels (or in some cases additional constraints and assumptions). However, this does not mean that model equivalences are limited to models within the same family. Indeed, the reparametrization operation can be considered both a source of model generalization and of model equivalence. The combination of the M2PM as given by Eq.  (9)  with the link functions  (19) and (20)  yields the general equations

                       which can be interpreted as ‘general frameworks’ or ‘general models’, once specific structures, link functions, and kernels have been chosen. By general frameworks we mean that seemingly different models, possibly defined on the basis of different assumptions on the conditional probabilities  
                         
                            P 
                            
                               ( 
                               X 
                               | 
                               K 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            π 
                            
                               ( 
                               K 
                               | 
                               C 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , might be reparametrized as differently constrained versions of the same choice of link function and kernel. This behavior is common in CDA, where general frameworks like GDINA, LCDM, or GDM (MGDM if a competence-based  
                         g 
                      -process is considered) correspond to specific choices of link function and kernel in Eqs.  (24) and (25) . Models belonging to different families in  Table 4  can be accommodated within such frameworks as long as they can be reparametrized in such a way that they result in differently constrained versions of the same functional form of the kernel, typically a linear one as in kernel  (23) , with the same choice of link function, typically a logit, a log, or the identity. However, this behavior also implies that models that might be apparently different and based on rather different hypotheses, might actually lead to equivalent distributions of responses. This phenomenon, has recently drawn attention in CDA (see, e.g.,  Köhn & Chiu, 2016; Maris & Bechger, 2008 ). More detailed CDA examples are discussed in Part II, but to provide a well-known example, consider the DINA model, here classified in  Table 4  under the assumption of a deterministic  
                         p 
                      -process, and the NIDA model, here classified in  Table 4  under the requirements of an effective competence state and of LI on the attributes in a probabilistic  
                         p 
                      -process. Both these models can be reparametrized as members of the general model provided by Eq.  (25)  under suitable constraints of the parameters in kernel  (23) . The choice of a logit link and of kernel  (23)  for instance yields the so-called LCDM framework. The choice of an identity link and of kernel  (23)  yields the so-called GDINA framework. Yet, not all models can be subsumed under the same general framework. For instance, a 4PL IRT model, which requires both the  
                         p 
                      - and  
                         g 
                      -process to be probabilistic, cannot be given the same parametrization as it would require a different kernel in Eq.  (25) , specifically a non-linear one. One might then interpret  Tables 4 and 5  as the lowest level of a hierarchical classification (as if there was a further dimension jutting from the page) in which models in different families are grouped according to the general models provided by Eqs.  (24) and (25)  and based on the equivalences established by specific choices of kernel and link function. These latter equivalences are not considered here, since the taxonomy is based on the most common choices for the  
                         p 
                      - and  
                         g 
                      -processes that can be traced in literature. A thorough study of these equivalences is beyond the scope of the present manuscript and, up to our knowledge, is an open problem in literature. For this reason, frameworks like LCDM, GDINA, GDM, and MGDM, and approaches like ECDM, which are in principle applicable to any family (although they are typically applied only to some of them with specific choices of link functions and kernels), are not listed in  Table 4 .

Some final remarks follow. The families in  Table 5  are general in nature and can be used to identify models and generalize situations currently not considered in  Table 4 . For instance, many IRT and CDA models implicitly assume structures to be chains (e.g., polytomous items and attributes) or power sets of items or attributes. One might however assume arbitrary structures in place of chains and power sets. Similarly, models for dichotomous items are classified in  Table 4  using a single-item structure in order to capture their IRFs, but more dichotomous items can be described considering suitable knowledge structures. Only if LI is assumed, multiple models for a single item are equivalent to a single model for multiple items, otherwise more general situations are modeled. In addition, left-side added models (either competence-based or not) can always be rewritten without left-side added parameters, and vice-versa. The families in  Tables 4 and 5  increase in generality from top to bottom with models listed at the top being nested within those at the bottom. For instance, a 4PL contains a 2PL, Samejima’s extension of the BNM contains the BNM, a LKS contains all IRT models listed above it in the same column, ability-based models like the 1PL-AG contain models like the Rasch model. Besides, at first glance, the nesting might appear to be not necessarily restricted to models in the same column. For instance, the 1PL-AG considers a Rasch model for the  
                         p 
                      -process  
                         
                            
                               π 
                            
                            
                               K 
                               | 
                               C 
                            
                         
                      , while MM-IRT models consider a Rasch model for the competence-based  
                         g 
                      -process  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               X 
                               | 
                               C 
                               , 
                               K 
                            
                         
                      , while keeping the  
                         p 
                      -process as competence-independent  
                         
                            
                               
                                  π 
                               
                               
                                  K 
                                  | 
                                  C 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            
                               
                                  π 
                               
                               
                                  K 
                               
                            
                         
                       (see Part III for a discussion). This off-column type of nesting might however be considered only apparent, as it occurs because different processes are given the same reparametrization thus leading to the same functional form. Finally, generalized versions of KST, CDA, and IRT models can be obtained in the bottom families in which models are currently not listed since we are at present unaware of models in such categories.

Earlier CDA models are derived according to the ‘conjunctive’ (e.g., NIDA, NC-RUM, MCLCM-C) or ‘disjunctive’ (MCLCM-D) behavior of the problem function. As modern CDA frameworks and IRT models involve reparametrizations of the conditional probabilities by means of link functions like Eq.  (25)  all these models can be given representation in the GDINA, LCDM, and GDM (or MGDM for competence-based  
                                     g 
                                  -processes) frameworks. The conjunctive or disjunctive nature of the problem functions corresponds, within kernel  (23) , to a choice of positive coefficients  
                                     
                                        
                                           λ 
                                        
                                        
                                           k 
                                           , 
                                           T 
                                        
                                     
                                   for the conjunctive problem function and to the existence of negative coefficients  
                                     
                                        
                                           λ 
                                        
                                        
                                           k 
                                           , 
                                           T 
                                        
                                     
                                   for the disjunctive problem one. Elimination of the interactions yields main effects models (NIDO, C-RUM, R-RUM, LLM, ACDM). See Part II for more details. These CDA models exemplify how, via reparametrization, the same general model can encompass models that belong to different families and are based on different assumptions.

In the probabilistic case, an important distinction is drawn based on whether some form of independence is assumed on the attributes or not. We consider here the case in which independence on attributes is not assumed (see point 4 below for the other case). A probabilistic  
                                     p

                                     g

The latter assumption captures whether the problem function is simultaneously applied to all items/thresholds, or to each item/threshold separately. The difference between these models lies in the choices of structures, kernels, and link functions. See Part III for a detailed derivation. In KST, the same case corresponds to an arbitrary ‘state response function’, which generalizes the idea of an IRF to knowledge states ( Noventa et al., 2019 ), while in CDA it corresponds to ‘main effects’ models (NIDO, C-RUM, R-RUM, LLM, ACDM) that are obtained by eliminating all interaction terms in kernel  (23)  so that only main effects are retained. These models are therefore discrete versions of multi-dimensional IRT models like the M2PL. See Part II for further details on KST and CDA models and Part III for their IRT counterparts. Earlier CDA models are derived according to the ‘conjunctive’ (e.g., NIDA, NC-RUM, MCLCM-C) or ‘disjunctive’ (MCLCM-D) behavior of the problem function. As modern CDA frameworks and IRT models involve reparametrizations of the conditional probabilities by means of link functions like Eq.  (25)  all these models can be given representation in the GDINA, LCDM, and GDM (or MGDM for competence-based  
                                     g 
                                  -processes) frameworks. The conjunctive or disjunctive nature of the problem functions corresponds, within kernel  (23) , to a choice of positive coefficients  
                                     
                                        
                                           λ 
                                        
                                        
                                           k 
                                           , 
                                           T 
                                        
                                     
                                   for the conjunctive problem function and to the existence of negative coefficients  
                                     
                                        
                                           λ 
                                        
                                        
                                           k 
                                           , 
                                           T 
                                        
                                     
                                   for the disjunctive problem one. Elimination of the interactions yields main effects models (NIDO, C-RUM, R-RUM, LLM, ACDM). See Part II for more details. These CDA models exemplify how, via reparametrization, the same general model can encompass models that belong to different families and are based on different assumptions.

As a consequence of the framework introduced in the present manuscript, KST, CDA, and IRT models can be identified within the taxonomy provided in  Table 4  by considering the five general questions given in the introduction and here reported for the sake of practicality.

Q1 
                            Which atomic elements and structures are involved? 
                         
                         
                            Q2 
                            How many processes are involved? 
                         
                         
                            Q3 
                            Which assumptions are imposed on the processes? 
                         
                         
                            Q4 
                            Is there some independence assumption on the processes? 
                         
                         
                            Q5 
                            Which link function and kernel are chosen?

Which atomic elements and structures are involved?

How many processes are involved?

Which assumptions are imposed on the processes?

Is there some independence assumption on the processes?

Which link function and kernel are chosen?

The KST concept of  structure  (Question 1) was introduced as a primitive to model hierarchies of atomic elements of different sorts (e.g., items, attributes, responses, and thresholds). Structures allow to capture order relations not only between items and attributes, but also to model more general relations between, e.g., sub-tasks or sub-attributes, as well as polytomous items and attributes. A second primitive, the concept of  process  (Question 2), was defined as a probabilistic relation between two structures and essentially recasts the probabilistic approach of GLVMs in a way that allows to introduce within it the concept of structure. The two primitives of structure and process can then be used to represent a combination of both probabilistic and deterministic relations between different atomic elements. In addition, processes can be combined both sequentially or in parallel, thus providing different approaches to probabilistic modeling. A particular configuration, a  two-processes model  was introduced, based on the sequential application of a  
                         p 
                      -process – which establish a relation between attributes and items thus allowing for educated guesses and slipping errors associated to attributes – and of a  
                         g 
                      -process – which establishes a relation between the mastering of the items and the actual response patterns thus allowing for blind guesses and mistakes. If the  
                         p 
                      -process is used to model traditional GLVMs, addition of a  
                         g 
                      -process extends these models to left-side added parameters as in  Thissen and Steinberg (1986) . Further extension to competence-based left side added parameters in the  
                         g 
                      -process (which corresponds to the situation in which the two-processes are not sequential and independent anymore) allows to model even more complex situations that have been recently considered in the psychometric literature like ability-based guessing parameters and Mixture-Measurement IRT models. The general framework suggested above should then be considered as an integration of traditional GLVMs with the KST set-theoretic approach, which allows to encompass KST, CDA, and IRT models, by capturing the relations between different structures via modeling of the conditional probabilities between the different layers of attributes, items, and responses (or thresholds in the case of polytomous items and attributes and latent traits, see Part III). In particular, the usually probabilistic approach of latent variable modeling is extended to encompass a deterministic relation between attributes and items at the core of the  
                         p 
                      -process. Such relation is modeled through the concept of a problem function that maps competence states into knowledge states. The problem function is rewritten as a  generalized  
                      
                         Q 
                      
                      -matrix , which extends the CDA concept of a  
                         Q 
                      -matrix to the relation between competence and knowledge states, rather than between attributes and items. An overview of the resulting taxonomy was given in  Tables 4 and 5  where, depending on how the conditional probabilities of the  
                         p 
                      -process and of the  
                         g 
                      -process were set (Question 3), the models can be subdivided in families that share a common blueprint, up to additional choices of structure, link function, and kernel. Among all possible assumptions that one can impose upon processes, a particularly relevant one which deserves to be singled out is the concept of independence (Question 4). A  factorization  operation was introduced as a decomposition of the conditional probabilities based on whether or not some form of independence (here given in a generalized version) was imposed on attributes and/or on items (for dichotomous items) or thresholds (for polytomous item and attributes or latent traits). A second operation,  reparametrization , (Question 5) allows to switch between different functional shapes of the same models by means of some analytic transformation captured by a choice of different link functions and kernels, and allows to construct general models and frameworks like those applied in CDA. Important common trends that emerge from the introduced framework are therefore that (a) KST and early CDA approaches appear to be based on the same theory applied to elements of different sorts and with a focus on factorized conditional probabilities, while (b) modern CDA approaches appear to be discrete versions of IRT models, with the latter built on the continuous version of finite structures, and with a focus on reparametrized conditional probabilities. The introduced framework is indeed used in Parts II and III to derive the KST, CDA, and IRT models listed in  Table 4 . It is shown there that the suggested kernel offers a simple way of classifying the CDA based on the conjunctive or disjunctive nature of the problem function. Most of all, the same kernel is also used to derive IRT models in Part III where latent traits are introduced as real-valued realizations of the random variables associated to uncountable linear orders of competence states. This allows to derive models for both dichotomous and polytomous items in both uni- and multi-dimensional settings.

Many-sorted structures and 1-sorted with multiple copies behave formally in the same way as they are all latent dimensions from a GLVMs perspective (see also Part III).
